<p>The <b>Louvre</b> or the <b>Louvre Museum</b> (<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr"><i>Musée du Louvre</i></span>, <small>pronounced: </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_French" title="Help:IPA for French">[myze dy luvʁ]</a></span>) (<small>French</small> <span class="nounderlines nowrap"><span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_pronunciation.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/83/Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_pronunciation.ogg" title="Musée du Louvre pronunciation.ogg"></a></span> <small class="metadata audiolinkinfo" style="cursor:help;">(<a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help"><span style="cursor:help;">help</span></a>·<a href="/wiki/File:Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_pronunciation.ogg" title="File:Musée du Louvre pronunciation.ogg"><span style="cursor:help;">info</span></a>)</small></span></span>) is the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_art_museums" title="List of largest art museums">largest</a> <a href="/wiki/Museum" title="Museum">museum</a> and a historic monument in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>. A central landmark of the city, it is located on the <a href="/wiki/Rive_Droite" title="Rive Droite">Right Bank</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Seine" title="Seine">Seine</a> in the city's <a href="/wiki/1st_arrondissement_of_Paris" title="1st arrondissement of Paris">1st arrondissement</a> (district or ward). Nearly 35,000 objects from prehistory to the 21st century are exhibited over an area of 72,735 square metres (782,910 square feet).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> The Louvre is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_most_visited_art_museums_in_the_world" title="List of most visited art museums in the world">world's second most visited museum</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_City" title="Forbidden City">Palace Museum</a> in China, receiving more than 9.26 million visitors in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArtnewsApril2015_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-ArtnewsApril2015-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The museum is housed in the <a href="/wiki/Louvre_Palace" title="Louvre Palace">Louvre Palace</a>, originally built as a fortress in the late 12th century under <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_France" title="Philip II of France">Philip II</a>. Remnants of the fortress are visible in the basement of the museum. Due to the urban expansion of the city, the fortress eventually lost its defensive function and, in 1546, was converted by <a href="/wiki/Francis_I_of_France" title="Francis I of France">Francis I</a> into the main residence of the French Kings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The building was extended many times to form the present Louvre Palace. In 1682, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_XIV" title="Louis XIV">Louis XIV</a> chose the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Versailles" title="Palace of Versailles">Palace of Versailles</a> for his household, leaving the Louvre primarily as a place to display the royal collection, including, from 1692, a collection of ancient Greek and Roman sculpture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> In 1692, the building was occupied by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acad%C3%A9mie_des_Inscriptions_et_Belles_Lettres" title="Académie des Inscriptions et Belles Lettres">Académie des Inscriptions et Belles Lettres</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acad%C3%A9mie_Royale_de_Peinture_et_de_Sculpture" title="Académie Royale de Peinture et de Sculpture">Académie Royale de Peinture et de Sculpture</a>, which in 1699 held the first of a series of salons. The Académie remained at the Louvre for 100 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Constituent_Assembly" title="National Constituent Assembly">National Assembly</a> decreed that the Louvre should be used as a museum to display the nation's masterpieces.</p>
<p>The museum opened on 10 August 1793 with an exhibition of 537 paintings, the majority of the works being royal and confiscated church property. Because of structural problems with the building, the museum was closed in 1796 until 1801. The collection was increased under <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> and the museum renamed the <i>Musée Napoléon</i>, but after Napoleon's abdication many works seized by his armies were returned to their original owners. The collection was further increased during the reigns of <a href="/wiki/Louis_XVIII_of_France" title="Louis XVIII of France">Louis XVIII</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_X_of_France" title="Charles X of France">Charles X</a>, and during the <a href="/wiki/Second_French_Empire" title="Second French Empire">Second French Empire</a> the museum gained 20,000 pieces. Holdings have grown steadily through donations and bequests since the <a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">Third Republic</a>. The collection is divided among eight curatorial departments: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egyptian_Antiquities" title="Egyptian Antiquities">Egyptian Antiquities</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near_Eastern_Antiquities" title="Near Eastern Antiquities">Near Eastern Antiquities</a>; <a href="/wiki/Greek_art" title="Greek art">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_art" title="Etruscan art">Etruscan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_art" title="Roman art">Roman Antiquities</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_Art" title="Islamic Art">Islamic Art</a>; <a href="/wiki/Sculpture" title="Sculpture">Sculpture</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decorative_Arts" title="Decorative Arts">Decorative Arts</a>; Paintings; Prints and Drawings.</p>
<p></p>
<div class="toc" id="toc">
<div id="toctitle">
<h2>Contents</h2>
</div>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_The_Louvre">History of The Louvre</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History of The Louvre">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="12th.E2.80.9320th_centuries">12th–20th centuries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 12th–20th centuries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval.2C_Renaissance.2C_and_Bourbon_palace">Medieval, Renaissance, and Bourbon palace</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Medieval, Renaissance, and Bourbon palace">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Donjon_chateau_louvre.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="599" data-file-width="801" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Donjon_chateau_louvre.JPG/220px-Donjon_chateau_louvre.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Donjon_chateau_louvre.JPG/330px-Donjon_chateau_louvre.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Donjon_chateau_louvre.JPG/440px-Donjon_chateau_louvre.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Donjon_chateau_louvre.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Below-ground portions of the medieval Louvre are still visible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_32_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_32-6">[6]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Louvre_Palace" title="Louvre Palace">Louvre Palace</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Louvre_Palace" title="Louvre Palace">Louvre Palace</a>, which houses the museum, was begun as a fortress by <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_France" title="Philip II of France">Philip II</a> in the 12th century to protect the city from possible Viking attacks, with remnants of this building still visible in the crypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_32_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_32-6">[6]</a></sup> Whether this was the first building on that spot is not known; it is possible that Philip modified an existing tower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-7">[7]</a></sup> According to the authoritative <i><a href="/wiki/Grand_Larousse_encyclop%C3%A9dique" title="Grand Larousse encyclopédique">Grand Larousse encyclopédique</a></i>, the name derives from an association with <a href="/wiki/Wolf_hunting" title="Wolf hunting">wolf hunting</a> den (via Latin: <i>lupus</i>, lower Empire: <i>lupara</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> In the 7th century, St. Fare, an abbess in Meaux, left part of her "Villa called Luvra situated in the region of Paris" to a monastery.;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> this territory probably did not correspond exactly to the modern site, however.</p>
<p>The Louvre Palace was altered frequently throughout the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>. In the 14th century, <a href="/wiki/Charles_V_of_France" title="Charles V of France">Charles V</a> converted the building into a residence and in 1546, <a href="/wiki/Francis_I_of_France" title="Francis I of France">Francis I</a> renovated the site in <a href="/wiki/French_Renaissance" title="French Renaissance">French Renaissance</a> style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards198_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards198-10">[10]</a></sup> Francis acquired what would become the nucleus of the Louvre's holdings, his acquisitions including <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Mona_Lisa" title="Mona Lisa">Mona Lisa</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mona_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mona-11">[11]</a></sup> After Louis XIV chose Versailles as his residence in 1682, constructions slowed; however, the move permitted the Louvre to be used as a residence for artists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards198_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards198-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the mid-18th century there were an increasing number of proposals to create a public gallery, with the art critic La Font de Saint-Yenne publishing, in 1747, a call for a display of the royal collection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carb_56_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carb_56-14">[14]</a></sup> On 14 October 1750, <a href="/wiki/Louis_XV_of_France" title="Louis XV of France">Louis XV</a> agreed and sanctioned a display of 96 pieces from the royal collection, mounted in the <i>Galerie royale de peinture</i> of the <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg_Palace" title="Luxembourg Palace">Luxembourg Palace</a>. A hall was opened by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Fran%C3%A7ois_Paul_Le_Normant_de_Tournehem" title="Charles François Paul Le Normant de Tournehem">Le Normant de Tournehem</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Abel_Fran%C3%A7ois_Poisson" title="Abel François Poisson">Marquis de Marigny</a> for public viewing of the <i>Tableaux du Roy</i> on Wednesdays and Saturdays, and contained <a href="/wiki/Andrea_del_Sarto" title="Andrea del Sarto">Andrea del Sarto</a>'s <i>Charity</i> and works by <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>; <a href="/wiki/Titian" title="Titian">Titian</a>; <a href="/wiki/Paolo_Veronese" title="Paolo Veronese">Veronese</a>; <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt" title="Rembrandt">Rembrandt</a>; <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Poussin" title="Nicolas Poussin">Poussin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Anthony_van_Dyck" title="Anthony van Dyck">Van Dyck</a>, until its closing in 1780 as a result of the gift of the palace to the <a href="/wiki/Louis_XVIII_of_France" title="Louis XVIII of France">Count of Provence</a> (the future king, Louis XVIII) by the king in 1778.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nora_278_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nora_278-15">[15]</a></sup> Under <a href="/wiki/Louis_XVI_of_France" title="Louis XVI of France">Louis XVI</a>, the royal museum idea became policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carb_56_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carb_56-14">[14]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles-Claude_Flahaut_de_la_Billaderie,_comte_d%27Angiviller" title="Charles-Claude Flahaut de la Billaderie, comte d'Angiviller">comte d'Angiviller</a> broadened the collection and in 1776 proposed conversion of the <i>Grande Galerie</i> of the Louvre – which contained maps – into the "French Museum".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nora_278_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nora_278-15">[15]</a></sup> Many proposals were offered for the Louvre's renovation into a museum; however, none was agreed on. Hence the museum remained incomplete until the French Revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nora_278_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nora_278-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="French_Revolution">French Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: French Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>During the French Revolution the Louvre was transformed into a public museum. In May 1791, <a href="/wiki/National_Constituent_Assembly" title="National Constituent Assembly">the Assembly</a> declared that the Louvre would be "a place for bringing together monuments of all the sciences and arts".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nora_278_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nora_278-15">[15]</a></sup> On 10 August 1792, <a href="/wiki/Louis_XVI_of_France" title="Louis XVI of France">Louis XVI</a> was imprisoned and the royal collection in the Louvre became national property. Because of fear of vandalism or theft, on 19 August, the National Assembly pronounced the museum's preparation as urgent. In October, a committee to "preserve the national memory" began assembling the collection for display.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Opening">Opening</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Opening">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amor-Psyche-Canova-JBU02.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3212" data-file-width="3209" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Amor-Psyche-Canova-JBU02.JPG/220px-Amor-Psyche-Canova-JBU02.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Amor-Psyche-Canova-JBU02.JPG/330px-Amor-Psyche-Canova-JBU02.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Amor-Psyche-Canova-JBU02.JPG/440px-Amor-Psyche-Canova-JBU02.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amor-Psyche-Canova-JBU02.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Antonio_Canova" title="Antonio Canova">Antonio Canova</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Psyche_Revived_by_Cupid%27s_Kiss" title="Psyche Revived by Cupid's Kiss">Psyche Revived by Cupid's Kiss</a></i> was commissioned in 1787, donated in 1824.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-French_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-French-17">[17]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The museum opened on 10 August 1793, the first anniversary of the monarchy's demise. The public was given free access on three days per week, which was "perceived as a major accomplishment and was generally appreciated".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The collection showcased 537 paintings and 184 objects of art. Three quarters were derived from the royal collections, the remainder from confiscated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%89migr%C3%A9s" title="Émigrés">émigrés</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Church</a> property (<i><a href="/wiki/Biens_nationaux" title="Biens nationaux">biens nationaux</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alex_24_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alex_24-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nave_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nave-20">[20]</a></sup> To expand and organize the collection, the Republic dedicated 100,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Livres" title="Livres">livres</a> per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nora_278_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-Nora_278-15">[15]</a></sup> In 1794, France's revolutionary armies began bringing pieces from Northern Europe, augmented after the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tolentino" title="Treaty of Tolentino">Treaty of Tolentino</a> (1797) by works from the Vatican, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Laoco%C3%B6n_and_His_Sons" title="Laocoön and His Sons">Laocoön and His Sons</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Apollo_Belvedere" title="Apollo Belvedere">Apollo Belvedere</a></i>, to establish the Louvre as a museum and as a "sign of popular sovereignty".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alex_24_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alex_24-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The early days were hectic; privileged artists continued to live in residence, and the unlabelled paintings hung "frame to frame from floor to ceiling".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alex_24_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Alex_24-19">[19]</a></sup> The structure itself closed in May 1796 due to structural deficiencies. It reopened on <a href="/wiki/Bastille_Day" title="Bastille Day">14 July</a> 1801, arranged chronologically and with new lighting and columns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alex_24_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-Alex_24-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Napoleon"><span id="Mus.C3.A9e_Napol.C3.A9on"></span><span id="Napoleon_I"></span>Napoleon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Napoleon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_I" title="Napoleon I">Napoleon I</a>, a northern wing paralleling the <i>Grande Galerie</i> was begun, and the collection grew through successful military campaigns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Invasion_of_Egypt_(1798)" title="French Invasion of Egypt (1798)">Egyptian campaign</a> of 1798–1801, Napoléon appointed the museum's first director, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dominique_Vivant_Denon" title="Dominique Vivant Denon">Dominique Vivant Denon</a>. In tribute, the museum was renamed the "Musée Napoléon" in 1803, and acquisitions were made of Spanish, Austrian, Dutch, and Italian works, either as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spoils_of_war" title="Spoils of war">spoils</a> or through treaties such as the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tolentino" title="Treaty of Tolentino">Treaty of Tolentino</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alderson_25_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alderson_25-23">[23]</a></sup> At the end of Napoleon's First Italian Campaign in 1797, the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Campo_Formio" title="Treaty of Campo Formio">Treaty of Campo Formio</a> was signed with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Count_Philipp_von_Cobenzl" title="Count Philipp von Cobenzl">Count Philipp von Cobenzl</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austrian_Monarchy" title="Austrian Monarchy">Austrian Monarchy</a>. This treaty not only marked the completion of Napoleon's conquest of Italy, but also the end of the first phases of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars" title="French Revolutionary Wars">French Revolutionary Wars</a>. Under this treaty, Italian cities were required to contribute pieces of art and patrimony to take part in Napoleon's "parades of booty" through Paris before being put into the Louvre Museum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plant.2C_p._36_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plant.2C_p._36-24">[24]</a></sup> One of the most famous pieces taken under this program was the <a href="/wiki/Horses_of_Saint_Mark" title="Horses of Saint Mark">Horses of Saint Mark</a>. The four antique bronze horses, which had adorned the basilica of San Marco in Venice after the sack of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> in 1204, were brought to Paris to reside atop Napoleon's Arc du Carrousel in Paris in 1797.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plant.2C_p._36_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Plant.2C_p._36-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several churches and palaces, including Saint Mark's Basilica, were looted by the French, which outraged the Italians and their artistic and cultural sensibilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> In 1797, the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tolentino" title="Treaty of Tolentino">Treaty of Tolentino</a> was signed by Napoleon, and two statues, the <i>Nile</i> and <i>Tiber</i>, were taken to Paris. These statues had previously been in the Vatican, and both were housed in the Louvre until 1815. After the defeat of Napoleon, the <i>Nile</i> was returned to Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> However, the <i>Tiber</i> remained in the Louvre Museum and can be seen in the collections today.</p>
<p>The Italian Peninsula was not the only region from which Napoleon took art. Under the Directory government of the 1790s, Napoleon (then a General) led an expedition to Egypt. The campaign was an expansionist effort on the part of the government, but the Directory had another goal to make Paris the center of art, science, and culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The Directory wanted France to assume responsibility for liberating the works of art they deemed in danger in order to protect and nationalize the heritage and culture of their subjects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> As a result, there were teams of artists and scientists who accompanied the armies into battle equipped with lists of paintings, sculptures, and other pieces of patrimony that would be collected, crated, and shipped back to France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dominique_Vivant_Denon" title="Dominique Vivant Denon">Dominique Vivant Denon</a> was Napoleon's art advisor, and accompanied him on the expedition to Egypt. Through his initiative, the <a href="/wiki/Valley_of_the_Kings" title="Valley of the Kings">Valley of the Kings</a> in Egypt was discovered and studied extensively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> As a result, he was later installed by Napoleon as the director of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_Napol%C3%A9on" title="Musée Napoléon">Musée Napoléon</a>, formerly the Louvre, cementing the status of the museum as a center for global patrimony and storehouse for cultural heritage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the most important discoveries made during Napoleon's campaign in Egypt was the <a href="/wiki/Rosetta_Stone" title="Rosetta Stone">Rosetta Stone</a>. It was discovered in 1799, and eventually led to the ability to decipher ancient hieroglyphs. Although the Rosetta Stone was discovered by the French, it actually never made it to the Louvre Museum. It was seized by British Forces following the defeat of Napoleon in Egypt and the subsequent signing of the Treaty of Alexandria in 1801.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> It is now on display at the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the French defeat at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Waterloo" title="Battle of Waterloo">Waterloo</a>, the works' former owners sought their return. The Louvre's administrators were loath to comply and hid many works in their private collections. In response, foreign states sent emissaries to London to seek help, and many pieces were returned, even some that had been restored by the Louvre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alderson_25_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alderson_25-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> In 1815 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_XVIII" title="Louis XVIII">Louis XVIII</a> finally concluded agreements with the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Empire" title="Austrian Empire">Austrian</a> government<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> for the keeping of pieces such as Veronese's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wedding_at_Cana" title="The Wedding at Cana">Wedding at Cana</a></i> which was exchanged for a large <a href="/wiki/Charles_Le_Brun" title="Charles Le Brun">Le Brun</a> or the repurchase of the <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Albani" title="Alessandro Albani">Albani</a> collection.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Restoration_and_Second_Empire">Restoration and Second Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Restoration and Second Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MG-Paris-Aphrodite_of_Milos_edited.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3888" data-file-width="2592" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/MG-Paris-Aphrodite_of_Milos_edited.jpg/170px-MG-Paris-Aphrodite_of_Milos_edited.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/MG-Paris-Aphrodite_of_Milos_edited.jpg/255px-MG-Paris-Aphrodite_of_Milos_edited.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/MG-Paris-Aphrodite_of_Milos_edited.jpg/340px-MG-Paris-Aphrodite_of_Milos_edited.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MG-Paris-Aphrodite_of_Milos_edited.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <i><a href="/wiki/Venus_de_Milo" title="Venus de Milo">Venus de Milo</a></i> was added to the Louvre's collection during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Louis_XVIII_of_France" title="Louis XVIII of France">Louis XVIII</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Bourbon_Restoration" title="Bourbon Restoration">Restoration</a> (1814–30), <a href="/wiki/Louis_XVIII_of_France" title="Louis XVIII of France">Louis XVIII</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_X_of_France" title="Charles X of France">Charles X</a> between them added 135 pieces at a cost of 720,000 francs and created the department of Egyptian antiquities curated by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Champollion" title="Jean-François Champollion">Champollion</a>, increased by more than 7,000 works with the acquisition of antiquities in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edme-Antoine_Durand" title="Edme-Antoine Durand">Edme-Antoine Durand</a>, the Egyptian collection of <a href="/wiki/Henry_Salt_(Egyptologist)" title="Henry Salt (Egyptologist)">Henry Salt</a> or the second collection former by <a href="/wiki/Bernardino_Drovetti" title="Bernardino Drovetti">Bernardino Drovetti</a>. This was less than the amount given for rehabilitation of <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Versailles" title="Palace of Versailles">Versailles</a>, and the Louvre suffered relative to the rest of Paris. After the creation of the <a href="/wiki/French_Second_Republic" title="French Second Republic">French Second Republic</a> in 1848, the new government allocated two million francs for repair work and ordered the completion of the <i>Galerie d'Apollon</i>, the <i>Salon Carré</i>, and the <i>Grande Galérie</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_52_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_52-37">[37]</a></sup> In 1861, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_III_of_France" title="Napoleon III of France">Louis-Napoléon Bonaparte</a> bought 11,835 artworks including 641 paintings, Greek gold and other antiquities of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Campana_collection" title="Campana collection">Campana collection</a>. During the <a href="/wiki/Second_French_Empire" title="Second French Empire">Second French Empire</a>, between 1852 and 1870, the French economy grew; by 1870 the museum had added 20,000 new pieces to its collections, and the <a href="/wiki/Pavillon_de_Flore" title="Pavillon de Flore">Pavillon de Flore</a> and the <i>Grande Galérie</i> were remodelled under architects <a href="/wiki/Louis_Visconti" title="Louis Visconti">Louis Visconti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hector_Lefuel" title="Hector Lefuel">Hector Lefuel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_52_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_52-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sacking_of_the_Louvre_in_1871">Sacking of the Louvre in 1871</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Sacking of the Louvre in 1871">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The socialist revolutionaries of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commune_of_Paris" title="Commune of Paris">Commune of Paris</a> heavily damaged and partially looted the Louvre in their two months of tenure in Paris. On 23 May 1871, during the suppression of the Paris Commune, 12 men under the orders of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jules_Bergeret&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jules Bergeret (page does not exist)">Jules Bergeret</a>, the former chief military commander of the Commune, set the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuileries" title="Tuileries">Tuileries</a> palace compound that included the Louvre on fire at 7 p.m., using petroleum, liquid tar and turpentine. The fire lasted 48 hours and thoroughly gutted the library and other sections of the Louvre which were entirely destroyed. Only the museum itself miraculously survived.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_Republic_and_World_Wars">Third Republic and World Wars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Third Republic and World Wars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-L15196,_Paris,_Besuch_Gerd_v._Rundstedt_im_Louvre.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="570" height="309" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-L15196%2C_Paris%2C_Besuch_Gerd_v._Rundstedt_im_Louvre.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-L15196%2C_Paris%2C_Besuch_Gerd_v._Rundstedt_im_Louvre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-L15196%2C_Paris%2C_Besuch_Gerd_v._Rundstedt_im_Louvre.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-L15196%2C_Paris%2C_Besuch_Gerd_v._Rundstedt_im_Louvre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-L15196%2C_Paris%2C_Besuch_Gerd_v._Rundstedt_im_Louvre.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-L15196%2C_Paris%2C_Besuch_Gerd_v._Rundstedt_im_Louvre.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-L15196,_Paris,_Besuch_Gerd_v._Rundstedt_im_Louvre.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Generalfeldmarschall <a href="/wiki/Gerd_von_Rundstedt" title="Gerd von Rundstedt">Gerd von Rundstedt</a> seen with a plaster model of the <a href="/wiki/Venus_de_Milo" title="Venus de Milo">Venus de Milo</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> while visiting the Louvre with the curator Alfred Merlin on 7 October 1940</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_French_Republic" title="Third French Republic">Third Republic</a> (1870–1940) the Louvre acquired new pieces mainly via donations and gifts. The <i>Société des Amis du Louvre</i> donated the <i><a href="/wiki/Piet%C3%A0_of_Villeneuve-l%C3%A8s-Avignon" title="Pietà of Villeneuve-lès-Avignon">Pietà of Villeneuve-lès-Avignon</a></i>, and in 1863 an expedition uncovered the sculpture <i><a href="/wiki/Winged_Victory_of_Samothrace" title="Winged Victory of Samothrace">Winged Victory of Samothrace</a></i> in the Aegean Sea. This piece, though heavily damaged, has been prominently displayed since 1884.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_70_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_70-39">[39]</a></sup> The 583-item <i>Collection La Caze</i> donated in 1869, included works by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste-Sim%C3%A9on_Chardin" title="Jean-Baptiste-Siméon Chardin">Chardin</a>; <a href="/wiki/Fragonard" title="Fragonard">Fragonard</a>; <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt" title="Rembrandt">Rembrandt</a> – such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bathsheba_at_Her_Bath" title="Bathsheba at Her Bath">Bathsheba at Her Bath</a></i> – and <i>Gilles</i> by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Antoine_Watteau" title="Jean-Antoine Watteau">Watteau</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_70_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_70-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Museum expansion slowed after World War I, and the collection did not acquire many significant new works; exceptions were <a href="/wiki/Georges_de_La_Tour" title="Georges de La Tour">Georges de La Tour</a>'s <i>Saint Thomas</i> and <a href="/wiki/Edmond_James_de_Rothschild" title="Edmond James de Rothschild">Baron Edmond de Rothschild's</a> (1845–1934) 1935 donation of 4,000 engravings, 3,000 drawings, and 500 illustrated books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nave_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nave-20">[20]</a></sup> During World War II the museum removed most of the art and hid valuable pieces. When Germany occupied the <a href="/wiki/Sudetenland" title="Sudetenland">Sudetenland</a>, many important artworks such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Mona_Lisa" title="Mona Lisa">Mona Lisa</a></i> were temporarily moved to the <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Chambord" title="Château de Chambord">Château de Chambord</a>. When war was formally declared a year later, most of the museum's paintings were sent there as well. Select sculptures such as <i><a href="/wiki/Winged_Victory_of_Samothrace" title="Winged Victory of Samothrace">Winged Victory of Samothrace</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Venus_de_Milo" title="Venus de Milo">Venus de Milo</a></i> were sent to the <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_de_Valen%C3%A7ay" title="Château de Valençay">Château de Valençay</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> On 27 August 1939, after two days of packing, truck convoys began to leave Paris. By 28 December, the museum was cleared of most works, except those that were too heavy and "unimportant paintings [that] were left in the basement".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> In early 1945, after the liberation of France, art began returning to the Louvre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Grand_Louvre_Pyramids">Grand Louvre Pyramids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Grand Louvre Pyramids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Louvre_Pyramid" title="Louvre Pyramid">Louvre Pyramid</a></div>
<p>By 1874, the Louvre Palace had achieved its present form of an almost rectangular structure with the Sully Wing to the east containing the square <i>Cour Carrée</i> and the oldest parts of the Louvre; and two wings which wrap the <i>Cour Napoléon</i>, the Richelieu Wing to the north and the Denon Wing, which borders the Seine to the south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> In 1983, French President <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Mitterrand" title="François Mitterrand">François Mitterrand</a> proposed, as one of his <a href="/wiki/Grands_Projets_of_Fran%C3%A7ois_Mitterrand" title="Grands Projets of François Mitterrand">Grands Projets</a>, the <b>Grand Louvre</b> plan to renovate the building and relocate the Finance Ministry, allowing displays throughout the building. Architect <a href="/wiki/I._M._Pei" title="I. M. Pei">I. M. Pei</a> was awarded the project and proposed a glass pyramid to stand over a new entrance in the main court, the <i>Cour Napoléon</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The pyramid and its underground lobby were inaugurated on 15 October 1988; the pyramid was completed in 1989. The second phase of the <i>Grand Louvre</i> plan, <a href="/wiki/La_Pyramide_Invers%C3%A9e" title="La Pyramide Inversée">La Pyramide Inversée</a> (The Inverted Pyramid), was completed in 1993. As of 2002, attendance had doubled since completion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biz_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Biz-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louvre_Museum_Wikimedia_Commons.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="6740" height="336" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Louvre_Museum_Wikimedia_Commons.jpg/800px-Louvre_Museum_Wikimedia_Commons.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Louvre_Museum_Wikimedia_Commons.jpg/1200px-Louvre_Museum_Wikimedia_Commons.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Louvre_Museum_Wikimedia_Commons.jpg/1600px-Louvre_Museum_Wikimedia_Commons.jpg 2x" width="800"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louvre_Museum_Wikimedia_Commons.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Louvre Palace and the Pyramid (by night)</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paris_July_2011-27a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2840" data-file-width="7685" height="296" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Paris_July_2011-27a.jpg/800px-Paris_July_2011-27a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Paris_July_2011-27a.jpg/1200px-Paris_July_2011-27a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Paris_July_2011-27a.jpg/1600px-Paris_July_2011-27a.jpg 2x" width="800"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paris_July_2011-27a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Louvre Palace and the Pyramid (by day)</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louvre_Pyramid.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/42/Louvre_Pyramid.jpg/220px-Louvre_Pyramid.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/42/Louvre_Pyramid.jpg/330px-Louvre_Pyramid.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/42/Louvre_Pyramid.jpg/440px-Louvre_Pyramid.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louvre_Pyramid.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Louvre_Pyramid" title="Louvre Pyramid">Louvre Pyramid</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <i>Musée du Louvre</i> contains more than 380,000 objects and displays 35,000 works of art in eight curatorial departments with more than 60,600 square metres (652,000 sq ft) dedicated to the permanent collection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The Louvre exhibits sculptures, <a href="/wiki/Objet_d%27art" title="Objet d'art">objets d'art</a>, paintings, drawings, and archaeological finds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nave_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nave-20">[20]</a></sup> It is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_the_most_visited_museums_in_the_world" title="List of the most visited museums in the world">world's second most visited museum</a>, averaging 15,000 visitors per day, 65 percent of whom are foreign tourists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biz_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Biz-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>After architects <a href="/wiki/Mario_Bellini" title="Mario Bellini">Mario Bellini</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rudy_Ricciotti" title="Rudy Ricciotti">Rudy Ricciotti</a> had won an international competition to create its new galleries for Islamic art, the new 3,000 sq m<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Islamic_art.2C_covered_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Islamic_art.2C_covered-48">[48]</a></sup> pavilion eventually opened in 2012, consisting of ground- and lower-ground-level interior spaces topped by a golden, undulating roof (fashioned from almost 9,000 steel tubes that form an interior web) that seems to float within the neo-Classical Visconti Courtyard in the middle of the Louvre's south wing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The galleries, which the museum had initially hoped to open by 2009, represent the first major architectural intervention at the Louvre since the addition of I.M. Pei's glass pyramid in 1989.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 5 February 2015 about one hundred archaeologists, protesting against commercial private involvement to protect France's heritage, blocked Louvre's ticket desks to facilitate free access to the museum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thelocal_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-thelocal-51">[51]</a></sup> At least one announcement reading "Free entrance offered by the archeologists" has been attached to the ticket desk and a number of people visited the museum free of charge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thelocal_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-thelocal-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mona_lisa_crowd.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1993" data-file-width="3000" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Mona_lisa_crowd.jpg/220px-Mona_lisa_crowd.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Mona_lisa_crowd.jpg/330px-Mona_lisa_crowd.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Mona_lisa_crowd.jpg/440px-Mona_lisa_crowd.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mona_lisa_crowd.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Mona_Lisa" title="Mona Lisa">Mona Lisa</a></i> is the Louvre's most popular attraction.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paris_-_Restoration_workshops_in_the_Louvre_-_2408.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2912" data-file-width="4630" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Paris_-_Restoration_workshops_in_the_Louvre_-_2408.jpg/220px-Paris_-_Restoration_workshops_in_the_Louvre_-_2408.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Paris_-_Restoration_workshops_in_the_Louvre_-_2408.jpg/330px-Paris_-_Restoration_workshops_in_the_Louvre_-_2408.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Paris_-_Restoration_workshops_in_the_Louvre_-_2408.jpg/440px-Paris_-_Restoration_workshops_in_the_Louvre_-_2408.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paris_-_Restoration_workshops_in_the_Louvre_-_2408.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Restoration workshops in the Louvre.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Louvre is owned by the French government; however, since the 1990s it has become more independent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sacre_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sacre-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAtimes_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-LAtimes-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Since 2003, the museum has been required to generate funds for projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAtimes_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-LAtimes-53">[53]</a></sup> By 2006, government funds had dipped from 75 percent of the total budget to 62 percent. Every year, the Louvre now raises as much as it gets from the state, about €122 million. The government pays for operating costs (salaries, safety and maintenance), while the rest – new wings, refurbishments, acquisitions – is up to the museum to finance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-55">[55]</a></sup> A further €3 million to €5 million a year is raised by the Louvre from exhibitions that it curates for other museums, while the host museum keeps the ticket money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-55">[55]</a></sup> As the Louvre became a point of interest in the book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Da_Vinci_Code" title="The Da Vinci Code">The Da Vinci Code</a></i> and the 2006 film based on the book, the museum earned $2.5 million by allowing filming in its galleries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> In 2008, the French government provided $180 million of the Louvre's yearly $350 million budget; the remainder came from private contributions and ticket sales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sacre_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sacre-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Louvre employs a staff of 2,000 led by Director <a href="/wiki/Jean-Luc_Martinez" title="Jean-Luc Martinez">Jean-Luc Martinez</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> who reports to the French Ministry of Culture and Communications. Martinez replaced <a href="/wiki/Henri_Loyrette" title="Henri Loyrette">Henri Loyrette</a> in April 2013. Under Loyrette, who replaced <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Rosenberg" title="Pierre Rosenberg">Pierre Rosenberg</a> in 2001, the Louvre has undergone policy changes that allow it to lend and borrow more works than before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAtimes_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-LAtimes-53">[53]</a></sup> In 2006, it loaned 1,300 works, which enabled it to borrow more foreign works. From 2006 to 2009, the Louvre lent artwork to the <a href="/wiki/High_Museum_of_Art" title="High Museum of Art">High Museum of Art</a> in Atlanta, Georgia, and received a $6.9 million payment to be used for renovations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAtimes_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-LAtimes-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2012, the Louvre and the <a href="/wiki/Fine_Arts_Museums_of_San_Francisco" title="Fine Arts Museums of San Francisco">Fine Arts Museums of San Francisco</a> announced a five-year collaboration on exhibitions, publications, art conservation and educational programming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The €98.5 million expansion of the Islamic Art galleries in 2012 received state funding of €31 million, as well as €17 million from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alwaleed_Bin_Talal_Foundation" title="Alwaleed Bin Talal Foundation">Alwaleed Bin Talal Foundation</a> founded by the eponymous Saudi prince. The republic of Azerbaijan, the Emir of Kuwait, the Sultan of Oman and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Mohammed_VI_of_Morocco" title="King Mohammed VI of Morocco">King Mohammed VI of Morocco</a> donated in total €26 million. In addition, the opening of the Louvre Abu Dhabi is supposed to provide €400 million over the course of 30 years for its use of the museum's prestigious brand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Islamic_art.2C_covered_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Islamic_art.2C_covered-48">[48]</a></sup> Loyrette has tried to improve weak parts of the collection through income generated from loans of art and by guaranteeing that "20% of admissions receipts will be taken annually for acquisitions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAtimes_53-4"><a href="#cite_note-LAtimes-53">[53]</a></sup> He has more administrative independence for the museum and achieved 90 percent of galleries to be open daily, as opposed to 80 percent previously. He oversaw the creation of extended hours and free admission on Friday nights and an increase in the acquisition budget to $36 million from $4.5 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sacre_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sacre-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAtimes_53-5"><a href="#cite_note-LAtimes-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Satellite_museums">Satellite museums</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Satellite museums">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Lens">Lens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Lens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Louvre-Lens" title="Louvre-Lens">Louvre-Lens</a></div>
<p>In 2004, French officials decided to build a satellite museum on the site of an abandoned coal pit in the former mining town of <a href="/wiki/Lens,_Pas-de-Calais" title="Lens, Pas-de-Calais">Lens</a> to relieve the crowded Paris Louvre, increase total museum visits, and improve the industrial north's economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gentleman_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gentleman-60">[60]</a></sup> Six cities were considered for the project: Amiens, Arras, Boulogne-sur-Mer, Calais, Lens, and Valenciennes. In 2004, French Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Jean-Pierre_Raffarin" title="Jean-Pierre Raffarin">Jean-Pierre Raffarin</a> chose Lens to be the site of the new building, called <i>Le Louvre-Lens</i>. Japanese architects <a href="/wiki/SANAA" title="SANAA">SANAA</a> were selected to design the Lens project in 2005. Museum officials predicted that the new building, capable of receiving about 600 works of art, would attract up to 500,000 visitors a year when it opened in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gentleman_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gentleman-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Abu_Dhabi">Abu Dhabi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Abu Dhabi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Louvre_Abu_Dhabi" title="Louvre Abu Dhabi">Louvre Abu Dhabi</a></div>
<p>In March 2007, the Louvre announced that a Louvre museum would be completed by 2016 in <a href="/wiki/Abu_Dhabi" title="Abu Dhabi">Abu Dhabi</a>. A 30-year agreement, signed by French Culture Minister <a href="/wiki/Renaud_Donnedieu_de_Vabres" title="Renaud Donnedieu de Vabres">Renaud Donnedieu de Vabres</a> and Sheik Sultan bin Tahnoon Al Nahyan, will establish the museum on Saadiyat Island in Abu Dhabi in exchange for €832,000,000 (US$1.3 billion). The Louvre Abu Dhabi, designed by the French architect <a href="/wiki/Jean_Nouvel" title="Jean Nouvel">Jean Nouvel</a> and the engineering firm of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buro_Happold" title="Buro Happold">Buro Happold</a>, will occupy 24,000 square metres (260,000 sq ft) and will be covered by a roof shaped like a flying saucer. France agreed to rotate between 200 and 300 artworks during a 10-year period; to provide management expertise; and to provide four temporary exhibitions a year for 15 years. The art will come from multiple museums, including the Louvre, the <a href="/wiki/Centre_Georges_Pompidou" title="Centre Georges Pompidou">Georges Pompidou Centre</a>, the Musée d'Orsay, Versailles, the <a href="/wiki/Guimet_Museum" title="Guimet Museum">Musée Guimet</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_Rodin" title="Musée Rodin">Musée Rodin</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_du_quai_Branly" title="Musée du quai Branly">Musée du quai Branly</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation">Conservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Conservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vista_exterior_del_Museo_del_Louvre.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Vista_exterior_del_Museo_del_Louvre.JPG/220px-Vista_exterior_del_Museo_del_Louvre.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Vista_exterior_del_Museo_del_Louvre.JPG/330px-Vista_exterior_del_Museo_del_Louvre.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Vista_exterior_del_Museo_del_Louvre.JPG/440px-Vista_exterior_del_Museo_del_Louvre.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vista_exterior_del_Museo_del_Louvre.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Louvre Palace</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2009, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minister_of_Culture_(France)" title="Minister of Culture (France)">Minister of Culture</a> <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Mitterrand" title="Frédéric Mitterrand">Frédéric Mitterrand</a> approved a plan that would have created a storage facility 30 km northwest of Paris to hold objects from the Louvre and two other national museums in Paris's flood zone, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_du_Quai_Branly" title="Musée du Quai Branly">Musée du Quai Branly</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_d%27Orsay" title="Musée d'Orsay">Musée d'Orsay</a>; the plan was later scrapped. In 2013, his successor <a href="/wiki/Aur%C3%A9lie_Filippetti" title="Aurélie Filippetti">Aurélie Filippetti</a> announced that the Louvre would move more than 250,000 works of art<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-62">[62]</a></sup> held in a 20,000 square metres (220,000 sq ft) basement storage area in <a href="/wiki/Li%C3%A9vin" title="Liévin">Liévin</a>; the cost of the project, estimated at €60 million, will be split between the region (49%) and the Louvre (51%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The Louvre will be the sole owner and manager of the store.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-62">[62]</a></sup> In July 2015, a team led by British firm <a href="/wiki/Rogers_Stirk_Harbour_%2B_Partners" title="Rogers Stirk Harbour + Partners">Rogers Stirk Harbour + Partners</a> was selected to design the complex, which will have light-filled work spaces under one vast, green roof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversial_acquisitions">Controversial acquisitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Controversial acquisitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Louvre is involved in controversies that surround cultural property seized under Napoleon I, as well as during World War II by the Nazis. During <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_occupation_of_France_during_World_War_II" title="German occupation of France during World War II">Nazi occupation</a>, thousands of artworks were stolen. But after the war, 61,233 articles of more than 150,000 seized artworks returned to France and were assigned to the Louvre's <i>Office des Biens Privés</i>. In 1949, it entrusted 2,130 unclaimed pieces (including 1,001 paintings) to the <i>Direction des Musées de France</i> in order to keep them under appropriate conditions of conservation until their restitution and meanwhile classified them as MNRs (<i>Musees Nationaux Recuperation</i> or, in English, the National Museums of Recovered Artwork). Some 10% to 35% of the pieces are believed to come from Jewish spoliations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> and until the identification of their rightful owners, which declined at the end of the 1960s, they are registered indefinitely on separate inventories from the museum's collections.</p>
<p>They were exhibited in 1946 and shown all together to the public during four years (1950–1954) in order to allow rightful claimants to identify their properties, then stored or displayed, according to their interest, in several French museums including the Louvre. From 1951 to 1965, about 37 pieces were restituted. Since November 1996, the partly illustrated catalogue of 1947–1949 has been accessible online and completed. In 1997, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Alain_Jupp%C3%A9" title="Alain Juppé">Alain Juppé</a> initiated the Mattéoli Commission, headed by <a href="/wiki/Jean_Matt%C3%A9oli" title="Jean Mattéoli">Jean Mattéoli</a>, to investigate the matter and according to the government, the Louvre is in charge of 678 pieces of artwork still unclaimed by their rightful owners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> During the late 1990s, the comparison of the American war archives, which had not been done before, with the French and German ones as well as two court cases which finally settled some of the heirs' rights (Gentili di Giuseppe and Rosenberg families) allowed more accurate investigations. Since 1996, the restitutions, according sometimes to less formal criteria, concerned 47 more pieces (26 paintings, with 6 from the Louvre including a then displayed Tiepolo), until the last claims of French owners and their heirs ended again in 2006.</p>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Serge_Klarsfeld" title="Serge Klarsfeld">Serge Klarsfeld</a>, since the now complete and constant publicity which the artworks got in 1996, the majority of the French Jewish community is nevertheless in favour of the return to the normal French civil rule of <i>prescription acquisitive</i> of any unclaimed good after another long period of time and consequently to their ultimate integration into the common French heritage instead of their transfer to foreign institutions like during World War II.</p>
<p>In June 2015, the Louvre had been accused of discriminating against Israeli students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>Napoleon's campaigns acquired Italian pieces by treaties, as war reparations, and Northern European pieces as spoils as well as some antiquities excavated in Egypt, though the vast majority of the latter were seized as war reparations by the British army and are now part of collections of the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>. On the other hand, the <a href="/wiki/Dendera_zodiac" title="Dendera zodiac">Dendera zodiac</a> is, like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rosetta_stone" title="Rosetta stone">Rosetta stone</a>, claimed by Egypt even though it was acquired in 1821, before the Egyptian Anti-export legislation of 1835. The Louvre administration has thus argued in favor of retaining this item despite requests by Egypt for its return. The museum participates too in arbitration sessions held via <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a>'s Committee for Promoting the Return of Cultural Property to Its Countries of Origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The museum consequently returned in 2009 five Egyptian fragments of frescoes (30 cm x 15 cm each) whose existence of the tomb of origin had only been brought to the authorities attention in 2008, eight to five years after their good-faith acquisition by the museum from two private collections and after the necessary respect of the procedure of <i>déclassement</i> from French public collections before the <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Commission_scientifique_nationale_des_collections_des_mus%C3%A9es_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Commission scientifique nationale des collections des musées de France (page does not exist)">Commission scientifique nationale des collections des musées de France</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Collections">Collections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Collections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <i>Musée du Louvre</i> contains more than 380,000 objects and displays 35,000 works of art in eight curatorial departments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Egyptian_antiquities">Egyptian antiquities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Egyptian antiquities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Egyptian_Antiquities_of_the_Louvre" title="Department of Egyptian Antiquities of the Louvre">Department of Egyptian Antiquities of the Louvre</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_seated_scribe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="622" data-file-width="577" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/The_seated_scribe.jpg/170px-The_seated_scribe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/The_seated_scribe.jpg/255px-The_seated_scribe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/The_seated_scribe.jpg/340px-The_seated_scribe.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_seated_scribe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/The_Seated_Scribe" title="The Seated Scribe">The Seated Scribe</a></i> from <a href="/wiki/Saqqara" title="Saqqara">Saqqara</a>, Egypt, limestone and alabaster, circa 2600 and 2350 BC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pocket_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pocket-72">[72]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The department, comprising over 50,000 pieces,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_74_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_74-73">[73]</a></sup> includes artifacts from the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> civilizations which date from 4,000 BC to the 4th century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nave_42_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nave_42-74">[74]</a></sup> The collection, among the world's largest, overviews Egyptian life spanning <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Middle Kingdom of Egypt">Middle Kingdom</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Kingdom" title="New Kingdom">New Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coptic_art" title="Coptic art">Coptic art</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Egypt" title="Roman Egypt">Roman</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Ptolemaic_Egypt" title="History of Ptolemaic Egypt">Ptolemaic</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nave_42_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nave_42-74">[74]</a></sup> The department's origins lie in the royal collection, but it was augmented by Napoleon's 1798 expeditionary trip with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dominique_Vivant" title="Dominique Vivant">Dominique Vivant</a>, the future director of the Louvre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_74_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_74-73">[73]</a></sup> After <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Champollion" title="Jean-François Champollion">Jean-François Champollion</a> translated the <a href="/wiki/Rosetta_Stone" title="Rosetta Stone">Rosetta Stone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_X_of_France" title="Charles X of France">Charles X</a> decreed that an Egyptian Antiquities department be created. Champollion advised the purchase of three collections, formed by <a href="/wiki/Edm%C3%A9-Antoine_Durand" title="Edmé-Antoine Durand">Edmé-Antoine Durand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Salt_(Egyptologist)" title="Henry Salt (Egyptologist)">Henry Salt</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bernardino_Drovet&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bernardino Drovet (page does not exist)">Bernardino Drovet</a>; these additions added 7,000 works. Growth continued via acquisitions by <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Mariette" title="Auguste Mariette">Auguste Mariette</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Museum" title="Egyptian Museum">Egyptian Museum</a> in Cairo. Mariette, after excavations at <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Egypt" title="Memphis, Egypt">Memphis</a>, sent back crates of archaeological finds including <i><a href="/wiki/The_Seated_Scribe" title="The Seated Scribe">The Seated Scribe</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_74_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_74-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Egyptian_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Egyptian-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Guarded by the <i>Large Sphinx</i> (c. 2000 BC), the collection is housed in more than 20 rooms. Holdings include art, <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a> scrolls, mummies, tools, clothing, jewelry, games, musical instruments, and weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_74_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_74-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nave_42_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nave_42-74">[74]</a></sup> Pieces from the ancient period include the <i><a href="/wiki/Gebel_el-Arak_Knife" title="Gebel el-Arak Knife">Gebel el-Arak Knife</a></i> from 3400 BC, <i>The Seated Scribe</i>, and the <i>Head of King Djedefre</i>. Middle Kingdom art, "known for its gold work and statues", moved from realism to idealization; this is exemplified by the <a href="/wiki/Schist" title="Schist">schist</a> statue of <a href="/wiki/Amenemhatankh" title="Amenemhatankh">Amenemhatankh</a> and the wooden <i>Offering Bearer</i>. The New Kingdom and Coptic Egyptian sections are deep, but the statue of the goddess <a href="/wiki/Nephthys" title="Nephthys">Nephthys</a> and the limestone depiction of the goddess <a href="/wiki/Hathor" title="Hathor">Hathor</a> demonstrate New Kingdom sentiment and wealth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nave_42_74-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nave_42-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Egyptian_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Egyptian-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Near_Eastern_antiquities">Near Eastern antiquities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Near Eastern antiquities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Human_headed_winged_bull_profile.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1438" data-file-width="1500" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Human_headed_winged_bull_profile.jpg/170px-Human_headed_winged_bull_profile.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Human_headed_winged_bull_profile.jpg/255px-Human_headed_winged_bull_profile.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Human_headed_winged_bull_profile.jpg/340px-Human_headed_winged_bull_profile.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Human_headed_winged_bull_profile.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Human-headed winged bull (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shedu" title="Shedu">shedu</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a>, limestone, 8th century BC.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Near Eastern antiquities, the second newest department, dates from 1881 and presents an overview of early Near Eastern civilization and "first settlements", before the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>. The department is divided into three geographic areas: the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> (Iraq), and Persia (<a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>). The collection's development corresponds to archaeological work such as <a href="/wiki/Paul-%C3%89mile_Botta" title="Paul-Émile Botta">Paul-Émile Botta</a>'s 1843 expedition to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khorsabad" title="Khorsabad">Khorsabad</a> and the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Sargon_II" title="Sargon II">Sargon II</a>'s palace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nave_42_74-4"><a href="#cite_note-Nave_42-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_119_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_119-76">[76]</a></sup> These finds formed the basis of the Assyrian museum, the precursor to today's department.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nave_42_74-5"><a href="#cite_note-Nave_42-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>The museum contains exhibits from <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a> and the city of <a href="/wiki/Akkad_(city)" title="Akkad (city)">Akkad</a>, with monuments such as the Prince of Lagash's <i><a href="/wiki/Stele_of_the_Vultures" title="Stele of the Vultures">Stele of the Vultures</a></i> from 2450 BC and the <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stele</a> erected by <a href="/wiki/Naram-Sin_of_Akkad" title="Naram-Sin of Akkad">Naram-Sin</a>, King of Akkad, to celebrate a victory over barbarians in the <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">Zagros Mountains</a>. The 2.25-metre (7.38 ft) <a href="/wiki/Code_of_Hammurabi" title="Code of Hammurabi">Code of Hammurabi</a>, discovered in 1901, displays <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonian_Law" title="Babylonian Law">Babylonian Laws</a> prominently, so that no man could plead their ignorance. The 18th-century BC mural of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Investiture_of_Zimrilim" title="Investiture of Zimrilim">Investiture of Zimrilim</a></i> and the 25th-century BC <i><a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Ebih-Il" title="Statue of Ebih-Il">Statue of Ebih-Il</a></i> found in the ancient city-state of <a href="/wiki/Mari,_Syria" title="Mari, Syria">Mari</a> are also on display at the museum.</p>
<p>The Persian portion of Louvre contains work from the archaic period, like the <i>Funerary Head</i> and the Persian <i>Archers of Darius I</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nave_42_74-6"><a href="#cite_note-Nave_42-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Near_East_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Near_East-77">[77]</a></sup> This section also contains rare objects from <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a> which were also lent to the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a> for its Ancient Persia exhibition in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greek.2C_Etruscan.2C_and_Roman">Greek, Etruscan, and Roman</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Greek, Etruscan, and Roman">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nike_of_Samothrake_Louvre_Ma2369_n2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3633" data-file-width="2400" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Nike_of_Samothrake_Louvre_Ma2369_n2.jpg/170px-Nike_of_Samothrake_Louvre_Ma2369_n2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Nike_of_Samothrake_Louvre_Ma2369_n2.jpg/255px-Nike_of_Samothrake_Louvre_Ma2369_n2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Nike_of_Samothrake_Louvre_Ma2369_n2.jpg/340px-Nike_of_Samothrake_Louvre_Ma2369_n2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nike_of_Samothrake_Louvre_Ma2369_n2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <i><a href="/wiki/Winged_Victory_of_Samothrace" title="Winged Victory of Samothrace">Nike of Samothrace</a></i> (Winged Victory), marble, circa 190 BC</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Greek, Etruscan, and Roman department displays pieces from the Mediterranean Basin dating from the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> to the 6th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreekER_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-GreekER-79">[79]</a></sup> The collection spans from the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Cyclades" title="History of the Cyclades">Cycladic period</a> to the decline of the Roman Empire. This department is one of the museum's oldest; it began with appropriated royal art, some of which was acquired under <a href="/wiki/Francis_I_of_France" title="Francis I of France">Francis I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nave_42_74-7"><a href="#cite_note-Nave_42-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_155_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_155-80">[80]</a></sup> Initially, the collection focused on marble sculptures, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Venus_de_Milo" title="Venus de Milo">Venus de Milo</a></i>. Works such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Apollo_Belvedere" title="Apollo Belvedere">Apollo Belvedere</a></i> arrived during the Napoleonic Wars, but these pieces were returned after Napoleon I's fall in 1815. In the 19th century, the Louvre acquired works including vases from the Durand collection, bronzes such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Borghese_Vase" title="Borghese Vase">Borghese Vase</a></i> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_nationale" title="Bibliothèque nationale">Bibliothèque nationale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pocket_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pocket-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreekER_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-GreekER-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>The archaic is demonstrated by jewellery and pieces such as the limestone <i><a href="/wiki/Lady_of_Auxerre" title="Lady of Auxerre">Lady of Auxerre</a></i>, from 640 BC; and the cylindrical <i><a href="/wiki/Hera" title="Hera">Hera</a> of Samos</i>, circa 570–560 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nave_42_74-8"><a href="#cite_note-Nave_42-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> After the 4th century BC, focus on the human form increased, exemplified by the <i><a href="/wiki/Borghese_Gladiator" title="Borghese Gladiator">Borghese Gladiator</a></i>. The Louvre holds masterpieces from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic" title="Hellenistic">Hellenistic</a> era, including The <i>Winged Victory of Samothrace</i> (190 BC) and the <i>Venus de Milo</i>, symbolic of classical art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_155_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_155-80">[80]</a></sup> The long <i>Galerie Campana</i> displays an outstanding collection of more than one thousand <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_pottery" title="Greek pottery">Greek potteries</a>. In the galleries paralleling the Seine, much of the museum's Roman sculpture is displayed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GreekER_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-GreekER-79">[79]</a></sup> The Roman portraiture is representative of that genre; examples include the portraits of <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Vipsanius_Agrippa" title="Marcus Vipsanius Agrippa">Agrippa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Annius_Verus" title="Marcus Annius Verus">Annius Verus</a>; among the bronzes is the Greek <i><a href="/wiki/Apollo_of_Piombino" title="Apollo of Piombino">Apollo of Piombino</a></i>.</p>
