<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_art">Islamic art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Islamic art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Casket_ivory_Louvre_UCAD4417.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1370" data-file-width="1850" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Casket_ivory_Louvre_UCAD4417.jpg/220px-Casket_ivory_Louvre_UCAD4417.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Casket_ivory_Louvre_UCAD4417.jpg/330px-Casket_ivory_Louvre_UCAD4417.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Casket_ivory_Louvre_UCAD4417.jpg/440px-Casket_ivory_Louvre_UCAD4417.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Casket_ivory_Louvre_UCAD4417.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Casket, ivory and silver, Muslim Spain, 966</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Islamic art collection, the museum's newest, spans "thirteen centuries and three continents".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Islam_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Islam-82">[82]</a></sup> These exhibits, comprising ceramics, glass, metalware, wood, ivory, carpet, textiles, and miniatures, include more than 5,000 works and 1,000 shards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ahlund_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ahlund-83">[83]</a></sup> Originally part of the decorative arts department, the holdings became separate in 2003. Among the works are the <i><a href="/wiki/Pyxis_of_al-Mughira" title="Pyxis of al-Mughira">Pyxide d'al-Mughira</a></i>, a 10th century ivory box from <a href="/wiki/Andalusia" title="Andalusia">Andalusia</a>; the <i>Baptistery of Saint-Louis</i>, an engraved brass basin from the 13th or 14th century <a href="/wiki/Mamluk_Sultanate_(Cairo)" title="Mamluk Sultanate (Cairo)">Mamluk</a> period; and the 10th century <i><a href="/wiki/Suaire_de_Saint-Josse" title="Suaire de Saint-Josse">Shroud of Saint-Josse</a></i> from Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_119_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_119-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Islam_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Islam-82">[82]</a></sup> The collection contains three pages of the <i><a href="/wiki/Shahnameh" title="Shahnameh">Shahnameh</a></i>, an epic book of poems by <a href="/wiki/Ferdowsi" title="Ferdowsi">Ferdowsi</a> in <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, and a Syrian metalwork named the <i>Barberini Vase</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ahlund_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ahlund-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sculpture">Sculpture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Sculpture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_works_in_the_Louvre" title="List of works in the Louvre">List of works in the Louvre</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louvre_Grab.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Louvre_Grab.JPG/220px-Louvre_Grab.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Louvre_Grab.JPG/330px-Louvre_Grab.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Louvre_Grab.JPG/440px-Louvre_Grab.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louvre_Grab.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Tomb of <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Pot" title="Philippe Pot">Philippe Pot</a>, governor of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burgundy_(region)" title="Burgundy (region)">Burgundy</a> under <a href="/wiki/Louis_XI_of_France" title="Louis XI of France">Louis XI</a>, by Antoine Le Moiturier</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:African_Art,_Yombe_sculpture,_Louvre.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3738" data-file-width="2670" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/African_Art%2C_Yombe_sculpture%2C_Louvre.jpg/120px-African_Art%2C_Yombe_sculpture%2C_Louvre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/African_Art%2C_Yombe_sculpture%2C_Louvre.jpg/180px-African_Art%2C_Yombe_sculpture%2C_Louvre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/African_Art%2C_Yombe_sculpture%2C_Louvre.jpg/240px-African_Art%2C_Yombe_sculpture%2C_Louvre.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:African_Art,_Yombe_sculpture,_Louvre.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Yombe_people" title="Yombe people">Yombe</a> sculpture, 19th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The sculpture department comprises work created before 1850 that does not belong in the Etruscan, Greek, and Roman department.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sculpture_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sculpture-84">[84]</a></sup> The Louvre has been a repository of sculpted material since its time as a palace; however, only ancient architecture was displayed until 1824, except for <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Dying_Slave" title="Dying Slave">Dying Slave</a></i> and <i>Rebellious Slave</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_397_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_397-85">[85]</a></sup> Initially the collection included only 100 pieces, the rest of the royal sculpture collection being at Versailles. It remained small until 1847, when Léon Laborde was given control of the department. Laborde developed the medieval section and purchased the first such statues and sculptures in the collection, <i><a href="/wiki/Childebert_I" title="Childebert I">King Childebert</a></i> and <i>stanga door</i>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_397_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_397-85">[85]</a></sup> The collection was part of the Department of Antiquities but was given autonomy in 1871 under <a href="/wiki/Louis_Courajod" title="Louis Courajod">Louis Courajod</a>, a director who organized a wider representation of French works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sculpture_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sculpture-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_397_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_397-85">[85]</a></sup> In 1986, all post-1850 works were relocated to the new Musée d'Orsay. The Grand Louvre project separated the department into two exhibition spaces; the French collection is displayed in the Richelieu wing, and foreign works in the Denon wing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sculpture_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sculpture-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>The collection's overview of French <a href="/wiki/Sculpture" title="Sculpture">sculpture</a> contains <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_art" title="Romanesque art">Romanesque</a> works such as the 11th-century <i>Daniel in the Lions' Den</i> and the 12th-century <i>Virgin of Auvergne</i>. In the 16th century, Renaissance influence caused French sculpture to become more restrained, as seen in <a href="/wiki/Jean_Goujon" title="Jean Goujon">Jean Goujon</a>'s bas-reliefs, and <a href="/wiki/Germain_Pilon" title="Germain Pilon">Germain Pilon</a>'s <i>Descent from the Cross</i> and <i>Resurrection of Christ</i>. The 17th and 18th centuries are represented by <a href="/wiki/Gian_Lorenzo_Bernini" title="Gian Lorenzo Bernini">Gian Lorenzo Bernini</a>'s 1640–1 <a href="/wiki/Bust_of_Cardinal_Richelieu" title="Bust of Cardinal Richelieu">Bust of Cardinal Richelieu</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Maurice_Falconet" title="Étienne Maurice Falconet">Étienne Maurice Falconet</a>'s <i>Woman Bathing</i> and <i>Amour menaçant</i>, and <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_and_Michel_Anguier" title="François and Michel Anguier">François Anguier</a>'s obelisks. <a href="/wiki/Neoclassicism" title="Neoclassicism">Neoclassical</a> works includes <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Canova" title="Antonio Canova">Antonio Canova</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Psyche_Revived_by_Cupid%27s_Kiss" title="Psyche Revived by Cupid's Kiss">Psyche Revived by Cupid's Kiss</a></i> (1787).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_397_85-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_397-85">[85]</a></sup> The 18th and 19th centuries are represented by the French sculptor <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Barye" title="Alfred Barye">Alfred Barye</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decorative_arts">Decorative arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Decorative arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saint_Blaise_Louvre_OAR504.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="1200" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Saint_Blaise_Louvre_OAR504.jpg/220px-Saint_Blaise_Louvre_OAR504.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Saint_Blaise_Louvre_OAR504.jpg/330px-Saint_Blaise_Louvre_OAR504.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Saint_Blaise_Louvre_OAR504.jpg/440px-Saint_Blaise_Louvre_OAR504.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saint_Blaise_Louvre_OAR504.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
French <a href="/wiki/Stained_glass" title="Stained glass">stained glass</a> panel, 13th century, depicting <a href="/wiki/Saint_Blaise" title="Saint Blaise">Saint Blaise</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Objet_d%27art" title="Objet d'art">Objets d'art</a></i> collection spans the time from the Middle Ages to the mid-19th century. The department began as a subset of the sculpture department, based on royal property and the transfer of work from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilique_Saint-Denis" title="Basilique Saint-Denis">Basilique Saint-Denis</a>, the burial ground of French monarchs that held the <i>Coronation Sword of the Kings of France</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nave_130_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nave_130-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_451_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_451-87">[87]</a></sup> Among the budding collection's most prized works were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pietre_dure" title="Pietre dure">pietre dure</a> vases and bronzes. The Durand collection's 1825 acquisition added "ceramics, enamels, and stained glass", and 800 pieces were given by Pierre Révoil. The onset of <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romanticism</a> rekindled interest in <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval" title="Medieval">Medieval</a> artwork, and the Sauvageot donation expanded the department with 1,500 middle-age and <a href="/wiki/Faience" title="Faience">faïence</a> works. In 1862, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Campana_collection" title="Campana collection">Campana collection</a> added gold jewelry and maiolicas, mainly from the 15th and 16th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_451_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_451-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>The works are displayed on the Richelieu Wing's first floor and in the Apollo Gallery, named by the painter Charles Le Brun, who was commissioned by Louis XIV (the Sun King) to decorate the space in a solar theme. The medieval collection contains the coronation crown of Louis XIV, <a href="/wiki/Charles_V_of_France" title="Charles V of France">Charles V's</a> sceptre, and the 12th century <i>porphyry vase</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The Renaissance art holdings include <a href="/wiki/Giambologna" title="Giambologna">Giambologna</a>'s bronze <i>Nessus and Deianira</i> and the tapestry <i>Maximillian's Hunt</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nave_130_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nave_130-86">[86]</a></sup> From later periods, highlights include <a href="/wiki/Madame_de_Pompadour" title="Madame de Pompadour">Madame de Pompadour</a>'s <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A8vres" title="Sèvres">Sèvres</a> vase collection and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_III_of_France" title="Napoleon III of France">Napoleon III</a>'s apartments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nave_130_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nave_130-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>In September 2000, the Louvre Museum dedicated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gilbert_R._Chagoury" title="Gilbert R. Chagoury">Gilbert Chagoury</a> and Rose-Marie Chagoury Gallery to display tapestries donated by the Chagourys, including a 16th-century six-part tapestry suite, sewn with gold and silver threads representing sea divinities, which was commissioned in Paris for <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Colbert,_Marquis_de_Seignelay" title="Jean-Baptiste Colbert, Marquis de Seignelay">Colbert de Seignelay</a>, Secretary of State for the Navy.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Painting">Painting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Painting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Catalog_of_paintings_in_the_Louvre_Museum" title="Catalog of paintings in the Louvre Museum">Catalog of paintings in the Louvre Museum</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mona_Lisa,_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci,_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="11146" data-file-width="7479" height="328" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Mona_Lisa%2C_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci%2C_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg/220px-Mona_Lisa%2C_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci%2C_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Mona_Lisa%2C_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci%2C_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg/330px-Mona_Lisa%2C_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci%2C_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Mona_Lisa%2C_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci%2C_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg/440px-Mona_Lisa%2C_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci%2C_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mona_Lisa,_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci,_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <i><a href="/wiki/Mona_Lisa" title="Mona Lisa">Mona Lisa</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>), oil on panel, 1503–1519, probably completed while the artist was at the court of <a href="/wiki/Francis_I_of_France" title="Francis I of France">Francis I</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The painting collection has more than 7,500 works<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> from the 13th century to 1848 and is managed by 12 curators who oversee the collection's display. Nearly two-thirds are by French artists, and more than 1,200 are Northern European. The Italian paintings compose most of the remnants of Francis I and Louis XIV's collections, others are unreturned artwork from the Napoleon era, and some were bought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_199_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_199-92">[92]</a></sup> The collection began with Francis, who acquired works from Italian masters such as <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> and brought Leonardo da Vinci to his court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mona_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mona-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paintings_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paintings-94">[94]</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>, the Royal Collection formed the nucleus of the Louvre. When the <i>d'Orsay</i> train station was converted into the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_d%27Orsay" title="Musée d'Orsay">Musée d'Orsay</a> in 1986, the collection was split, and pieces completed after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1848_Revolution" title="1848 Revolution">1848 Revolution</a> were moved to the new museum. French and Northern European works are in the Richelieu wing and <i>Cour Carrée</i>; Spanish and Italian paintings are on the first floor of the Denon wing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_199_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_199-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>Exemplifying the French School are the early <i><a href="/wiki/Piet%C3%A0_of_Villeneuve-l%C3%A8s-Avignon" title="Pietà of Villeneuve-lès-Avignon">Avignon Pietà</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Enguerrand_Quarton" title="Enguerrand Quarton">Enguerrand Quarton</a>; the anonymous painting of <i>King Jean le Bon</i> (c.1360), possibly the oldest independent portrait in Western painting to survive from the postclassical era;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hyacinthe_Rigaud" title="Hyacinthe Rigaud">Hyacinthe Rigaud</a>'s <i>Louis XIV</i>; <a href="/wiki/Jacques-Louis_David" title="Jacques-Louis David">Jacques-Louis David</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Coronation_of_Napoleon" title="The Coronation of Napoleon">The Coronation of Napoleon</a></i>; and <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix" title="Eugène Delacroix">Eugène Delacroix</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Liberty_Leading_the_People" title="Liberty Leading the People">Liberty Leading the People</a></i>. Northern European works include <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Vermeer" title="Johannes Vermeer">Johannes Vermeer</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lacemaker_(Vermeer)" title="The Lacemaker (Vermeer)">The Lacemaker</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Astronomer_(Vermeer)" title="The Astronomer (Vermeer)">The Astronomer</a></i>; <a href="/wiki/Caspar_David_Friedrich" title="Caspar David Friedrich">Caspar David Friedrich</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tree_of_Crows" title="The Tree of Crows">The Tree of Crows</a></i>; <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt" title="Rembrandt">Rembrandt</a>'s <i>The Supper at Emmaus</i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bathsheba_at_Her_Bath" title="Bathsheba at Her Bath">Bathsheba at Her Bath</a></i>, and <i>The Slaughtered Ox</i>.</p>
<p>The Italian holdings are notable, particularly the Renaissance collection. The works include <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Mantegna" title="Andrea Mantegna">Andrea Mantegna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Bellini" title="Giovanni Bellini">Giovanni Bellini</a>'s <i>Calvary</i>s, which reflect realism and detail "meant to depict the significant events of a greater spiritual world".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> The High Renaissance collection includes Leonardo da Vinci's <i><a href="/wiki/Mona_Lisa" title="Mona Lisa">Mona Lisa</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Virgin_and_Child_with_St._Anne_(Leonardo_da_Vinci)" title="The Virgin and Child with St. Anne (Leonardo da Vinci)">Virgin and Child with St. Anne</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/St._John_the_Baptist_(Leonardo)" title="St. John the Baptist (Leonardo)">St. John the Baptist</a></i>, and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madonna_of_the_Rocks" title="Madonna of the Rocks">Madonna of the Rocks</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Caravaggio" title="Caravaggio">Caravaggio</a> is represented by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fortune_Teller_(Caravaggio)" title="The Fortune Teller (Caravaggio)">The Fortune Teller</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Death_of_the_Virgin_(Caravaggio)" title="Death of the Virgin (Caravaggio)">Death of the Virgin</a></i>. From 16th century Venice, the Louvre displays <a href="/wiki/Titian" title="Titian">Titian</a>'s <i>Le Concert Champetre</i>, <i>The Entombment</i> and <i>The Crowning with Thorns</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trois_t%C3%AAtes_d%27hommes_en_relation_avec_le_lion.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="393" data-file-width="585" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Trois_t%C3%AAtes_d%27hommes_en_relation_avec_le_lion.jpg/220px-Trois_t%C3%AAtes_d%27hommes_en_relation_avec_le_lion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Trois_t%C3%AAtes_d%27hommes_en_relation_avec_le_lion.jpg/330px-Trois_t%C3%AAtes_d%27hommes_en_relation_avec_le_lion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Trois_t%C3%AAtes_d%27hommes_en_relation_avec_le_lion.jpg/440px-Trois_t%C3%AAtes_d%27hommes_en_relation_avec_le_lion.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trois_t%C3%AAtes_d%27hommes_en_relation_avec_le_lion.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Three lion-like heads, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_le_Brun" title="Charles le Brun">Charles le Brun</a>, France, pen and wash on squared paper, 1671</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The La Caze Collection, a bequest to the Musée du Louvre in 1869 by <a href="/wiki/Louis_La_Caze" title="Louis La Caze">Louis La Caze</a>, was the largest contribution of a person in the history of the Louvre. La Caze gave 584 paintings of his personal collection to the museum. The bequest included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antoine_Watteau" title="Antoine Watteau">Antoine Watteau</a>'s Commedia dell'arte player of Pierrot ("Gilles"). In 2007, this bequest was the topic of the exhibition "1869: Watteau, Chardin... entrent au Louvre. La collection La Caze".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some of the best known paintings of the museum have been digitized by the French Center for Research and Restoration of the Museums of France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prints_and_drawings">Prints and drawings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Prints and drawings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Old_master_print" title="Old master print">prints</a> and drawings department encompasses works on paper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_495_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_495-101">[101]</a></sup> The origins of the collection were the 8,600 works in the Royal Collection (<i>Cabinet du Roi</i>), which were increased via state appropriation, purchases such as the 1,200 works from Fillipo Baldinucci's collection in 1806, and donations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pocket_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pocket-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drawing_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Drawing-102">[102]</a></sup> The department opened on 5 August 1797, with 415 pieces displayed in the <i>Galerie d'Apollon</i>. The collection is organized into three sections: the core <i>Cabinet du Roi</i>, 14,000 royal copper printing-plates, and the donations of <a href="/wiki/Edmond_James_de_Rothschild" title="Edmond James de Rothschild">Edmond de Rothschild</a>, which include 40,000 prints, 3,000 drawings, and 5,000 illustrated books. The holdings are displayed in the Pavillon de Flore; due to the fragility of the paper medium, only a portion are displayed at one time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mignot_495_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mignot_495-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Location.2C_access.2C_and_facilities">Location, access, and facilities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Louvre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Location, access, and facilities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louvre_Access_Map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="2000" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Louvre_Access_Map.png/220px-Louvre_Access_Map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Louvre_Access_Map.png/330px-Louvre_Access_Map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Louvre_Access_Map.png/440px-Louvre_Access_Map.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louvre_Access_Map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of Louvre museum and around, showing bus stops and metro lines serving the area as well as parking.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The museum lies in the center of Paris on the Right Bank. The neighborhood, known as the 1st arrondissement, was home to the former <a href="/wiki/Tuileries_Palace" title="Tuileries Palace">Tuileries Palace</a>, which closed off the western end of the Louvre entrance courtyard, but was heavily damaged by fire during the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Commune" title="Paris Commune">Paris Commune</a> of 1871 and later demolished. The adjacent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuileries_Gardens" title="Tuileries Gardens">Tuileries Gardens</a>, created in 1564 by Catherine de' Medici, was designed in 1664 by <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Le_N%C3%B4tre" title="André Le Nôtre">André Le Nôtre</a>. The gardens house the <a href="/wiki/Galerie_nationale_du_Jeu_de_Paume" title="Galerie nationale du Jeu de Paume">Galerie nationale du Jeu de Paume</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_art" title="Contemporary art">contemporary art</a> exhibition space which was used to store confiscated Jewish cultural property during the 1940 to 1944 German occupation of France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frommer_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frommer-103">[103]</a></sup> Parallel to the Jeu de Paume is the Orangerie, home to the famous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_Lilies" title="Water Lilies">Water Lilies</a> paintings by <a href="/wiki/Claude_Monet" title="Claude Monet">Claude Monet</a>.</p>
<p>The Louvre is slightly askew of the Historic Axis (<i><a href="/wiki/Axe_historique" title="Axe historique">Axe historique</a></i>), a roughly eight-kilometre (five-mile) architectural line bisecting the city. It begins on the east in the Louvre courtyard and runs west along the <a href="/wiki/Champs-%C3%89lys%C3%A9es" title="Champs-Élysées">Champs-Élysées</a>. In 1871, the burning of the Tuileries Palace by the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Commune" title="Paris Commune">Paris Commune</a> revealed that the Louvre was slightly askew of the <i>Axe</i> despite past appearances to the contrary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The Louvre can be reached by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palais_Royal_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_(Paris_M%C3%A9tro)" title="Palais Royal - Musée du Louvre (Paris Métro)">Palais Royal – Musée du Louvre</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paris_Metro" title="Paris Metro">Métro</a> or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louvre_-_Rivoli_(Paris_M%C3%A9tro)" title="Louvre - Rivoli (Paris Métro)">Louvre-Rivoli</a> stations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Louvre has three entrances: the main entrance at the pyramid, an entrance from the <a href="/wiki/Carrousel_du_Louvre" title="Carrousel du Louvre">Carrousel du Louvre</a> underground shopping mall, and an entrance at the Porte des Lions (near the western end of the Denon wing).</p>
<p>Under the main entrance to the museum is the <a href="/wiki/Carrousel_du_Louvre" title="Carrousel du Louvre">Carrousel du Louvre</a>, a shopping mall operated by <a href="/wiki/Unibail-Rodamco" title="Unibail-Rodamco">Unibail-Rodamco</a>. Among other stores, it has the first Apple Store in France, and a <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s" title="McDonald's">McDonald's</a> restaurant, the presence of which has created controversy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>The use of cameras and video recorders is permitted inside, but flash photography is forbidden.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

According to Mignot, <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Mantegna" title="Andrea Mantegna">Mantegna</a>'s <i>Calvary</i>, <a href="/wiki/Paolo_Veronese" title="Paolo Veronese">Veronese</a>'s <i>The Wedding at Cana|The Marriage of Cana</i>, and Rogier van der Weyden's <i>Annunciation</i> were not returned.</span></li>
